This video made me cry.  You're going to crush the cables that are connected to the copper SFPs when you close the door.  You always have to move the vertical rails back at least 5cm on the port side of the switches.  The bend radius on copper Ethernet cables is usually about 4x cable diameter - so for your ultra thin CAT6A (typically around 4mm diameter) you shouldn't have any bends tighter than 16mm (bit over 1/2 an inch).  Smooshing the door on them like that will cause issues - possibly immediately but certainly over time.  Just realised it's the 100G to 4x 25G breakout cable  that's being pinched.  That will have a minimum bend radius too.   This equally applies to fiber.  If you bend the fiber too tight several things happen.  Light starts to leak out from the core/cladding interface instead of being internally reflected and you end up with significant signal loss.  Otherwise - love the channel.  Very entertaining :)
Where‚Äôs your firewall?
Networking guy here. Now I understand the cringe that electricians feel when they see DIY'ers doing electrical.   That switch might have 48 POE ports but watch how many watts your cameras and WAPs use. Most POE switches start causing craziness above 24 devices.  Buy cable in bulk and make cables to the perfect length? Not so messy and much cheaper. It also makes pulling the cable much easier.  Hinged patch panels or ones that can pop out the front can be a life saver when you need to change that one cable that is between two switches.  Label maker... DYMO makes some just for cable management. It also works well to have a system. This is patch panel A with 1-48. Back at the workspace you label the cover plate A-1 A-2. You know those two cables are on patch panel A on 1 and 2.

Any data center guys take note of those thin patch cables, because they are a life saver.  We use the Monoprice ones by the thousands, and with PoE.  They are roughly half the diameter of a Cat6 cable, so quick maff says one quarter the volume in the wire management.  On Amazon we average about $1/ea, so the price is definitely right as well.

Is there a reason they did not use a wider rack? They are using a standard server rack (24" wide) Its really useful to have a few inches to the left and right of the vertical nema rails. Normally we would be racking up networking gear in 30" wide cabinets. Also, I mentioned below but they should have moved back the front rails to get plenty of space where the patching is going to be and allow them to close the front door. They appear to have brought the cabling in through the far side of the cabinet rather than the roof of the cabinet. A big bundle of cabling like that should have been routed inside the room using an overhead cable tray. 10Gb copper networking (not DAC like they used from their TOR switch) uses quite  lot of power, between 2 and 5 watts per port. Optical would be lower power and would have given the option of 25Gb just by changing the sfp later.

It's a good idea to use at least a 70 cm wide rack for network racks like this. Some will even argue for an 80 cm wide rack. The sides can accomodate more cables and it's easier to add new cables once the rack is populated. Avoid putting the rack against the wall, as access to the sides will help maintain cables for it later.  Usually the back 19" bracket will be recessed further into the rack so that switches can be placed in the back and have plenty of room for cables and SFP's to stick out. Think about PDU placement so power plugs do not hinder the removal of equipment once the rack is populated, if you use the back side. Mounting switches on the front, you would usually move the brackets back a bit to allow for the same effect. Put storage in another rack besides the network rack - it will make everything much easier and be less of a pain to expand. Clever things those rack studs. Also look into a labelwriter that prints on self lamenating labels. The text field will be protected by the clear end of the label, and never fall off or smudge. Edit: There is an installation tool for cage nuts.

just fyi you're installing racknuts wrong. Don't get me wrong, they still absolutely suck, but if you find a small thin piece of metal(think like butter knife) it makes it super easy to install them. You put one side of the racknut in, then insert the piece of metal on the other side and push the cage nut in, then remove the piece of metal. So you're sliding the other side of the racknut in using the piece of metal. It makes it SUPER easy.   I used to build servers for oracle and we could install a few hundred cage nuts in a minute or two using this metal. It still sucks to remove them.
With the metal threaded clips, pick up a couple of 4"/100mm bolts of the same thread, you can screw the bolts into the clip and use it as a handle for fitting the clip. takes a couple of sec's to screw in/out but can still be quicker - and safer.  Would it be worthwhile having drums of different coloured cable, that can be allocated to specific purposes rather than rely on take on the ends, it may make sorting them out from the tangles easier? Having the means - cables, fittings, tools, etc, to make your own cables may even be cheaper, neater and easier as you can make them the lengths actually required - especially if they're checked at the time of construction to make sure there isn't a problem with them that will come back to cause problems later.

Highly recommend a storage server in the rack room.  Data replication "on site" but in a different building is important in case you have another "our server literally caught fire" scenario again.  You'll be able to sync very fast because of the building to building setup during downtime periods (like overnight), but during the day if the guys in the building don't have to compete with  other data being transferred between the buildings to the main internet connection, it'll make everyone happier.  Besides, you know you want to do another petabyte project server.  >D
You guys did a pretty descent job, especially for non-proffesionals. But I suggest you check out the TI-568. It won't tell you specifically how to do things, but it is the National standard in the U.S. (plus it's a super easy certification for whoever wants it). Really while I have a few minor issues, really just experience things, I do have one gripe. I'm not a fan of just dropping the cables out of an open Ceiling Tile grid hole. I've seen more then enough times what that does long term, and what a mess it starts to create as you add I the future. I'd give you a more exact solution (who knows maybe you already got one), but its kind of hard based off a video where I can't see any of the building structure. Maybe you drop down some bridal rings. You could also remove the cables and then run them through big sleeves, or a vertical Cable tray. Honestly there's allot of acceptable solutions.

I've been in the IT field for many years and one of the worst network cabinets I've ever seen in my life was at a Lowes.  I had a ticket for a secondary switch that went down over the weekend at this Lowes. The replacement was sent to the location and I unpacked it thinking it wouldn't give me such an major issue. Boy, when I opened the door, cables exploded everywhere. The cables was so badly tangled that I almost started to cry,lol. I had to keep one switch up while replacing the other(done during business hours), but it seemed impossible.   I had to label one cable after another and hope that when I disconnected the switch, it would be easy to snap in the correct cables back into the replacement. Switches typically had 24 ports if it was cisco. But no, the cables from the primary switch was blocking the defective one. Imagine trying to squeeze out a switch that was literally almost the width of the cabinet, no wiggle room. I honestly don't remember how I completed the ticket. I think I blacked out when I got home.   I've seen some of the worst network cabinets at major retail stores and banks and somehow I don't see how those places function with such a messy set up. Some network set ups were so antiquated, replacements or parts that worked were hard to come by. Nothing new, just refurbished and possibly breaking down soon as you leave, or, hell, dead on arrive(DOA). Ugh....   A few years later, I changed my career and became a software engineer. I refused to go back into that nightmare of being an On-call Break-fix Field Tech.

The company I worked for many moons ago connected a remote building on the same grounds as one of our major hospitals at the time (the hospital is now a corporate setup.) with a 10 MB microwave setup. At the time, the speed was enough for what they were doing. HOWEVER, every little wind gust and stray pigeon became a headache for the people relying on the setup to do their jobs. Eventually the networking folks just gave up and ran fiber to the building. I know all of this, because I was working in the IT Service Desk at the time, so I was part of the staff that got the calls after every little event that either blocked line of sight or knocked the dishes out of alignment.  Fun Fact: The break area/smoking area for that office building was directly under the microwave link. Remember the pigeons I was talking about? Periodically, staff would be treated to a rain of barbequed pigeon while enjoying their break area.

Tips from a senior network engineer: Use 1RU 48port high density patch panels all mounted at top of rack. Label panels individually so jack labels are either A1 for panel A jack 1, or 1-1 etc. Rack all switches below leaving space between panels and switches so you can always install more panels and actually access them without the switches in the way. Also let's you replace switches without removing existing ones. Props for using 28ga patch cables. Use vertical wire managers. Only use horizontals to route cables from adjacent racks in which case one at the top and one in the middle of the racks.
19:50 Those cabinets are adjustable so the switches can be further back from the door so you aren't squashing the cables...

You guys should consider installing a backup server. It has the advantage of using local network resources to do something like hourly or sooner backups (replication or something like that) and if something happens to the primary server, allows to either run off the backup server over the air fiber, setting up temporary work stations in the building the backup server is in, or even moving the backup server to the main building to replace the main server. Its not a off site backup (it would be more like a semi-onsite backup) but it would allow for rapid recovery in the event something goes wrong with the main server.

Sorry for the long comment in advance.  To reduce stress on the network (I'm sure you have already done this). I suggest offloading L3 from the router to your switches and then set your subnets to a smaller scope, so away from \16 or \24 (if possible) and move to /25 - /28, usually for things like management ports and what not. As this helps reduce overall stress on the network. Though the existing network (my guess) is that it's not too intensive on the L3 side and more stressed on the L2 side of things.   Also, I'd be very curious to see if you would visit (or revisit if you have done this already) ReFS for files with Windows server 2019/2022 and see how it stacks up for general file server use in terms of performance & resiliency compared to ZFS. I only request it as you have a well sized environment in both endpoints and data and could show others how far ReFS has come in the past few years (compared to when it was first released in Server 2012).   Also, quick question, what do you guys use for your gateways (if you can share)? I'm curious as I'm potentially moving away from the UDM-P, but I wanted to get your thoughts as you have a much much larger deployment.
